Nice design feature.    The handle is an inch wide loop of material which is secured on one end by a hook-and-loop strap, and can also be unfastened and used just as a regular wrist strap. When fastened down with the hook-and-loop strap, the case can be put on a belt or pack strap as well. I am a bit leery of trusting hook-and-loop fasteners too much, so the ability to slide a belt through the handle itself makes me feel better about carrying the case that way. It should be quite secure even if the hooks let go or are pulled apart.    The case is made of two different kinds of nylon fabric, a Cordura-type, and a shinier, slicker type. It seems quite well made and sturdy.    All in all, it is a good, solid case for minimal gear carry.

Listeners looking for another Oasis record may find<br />themselves disappointed at the lack of thought-provoking commentary hidden in the lyrics, but those<br />willing to accept Beady Eye as an entirely different band will likely be drawn into the music. 1439102724|5.0|FIRST SENTENCE:  One sunny, crisp Saturday in September when I was seven years old, I watched my father drop dead.<br /><br />As I sit here listening to the very mellow CD that comes as a bonus with this book (a song corresponding to each chapter), I'm still blown away by this book.  So much so that I'm ignoring my planned review schedule to let people know about it.<br /><br />The publisher's description, well, it doesn't capture this book .. at all, really.  This book is about so much more than reproductive issues or same-sex marriage or any of that. I mean, it's in there; it's the premise of the book, but ... wow ... I was totally blown away by this one.  A book that makes you laugh AND cry?  Even thinking about it afterward brings a tear or two to my eye.  A little more on the plot (and I promised myself that I wouldn't put spoilers in, because many of the happenings that really affected me would qualify as such).<br /><br />Zoe is in her forties, a music therapist married to Max, a landscaper. They both have infertility issues, and have been unable to conceive naturally.  After 4 unsuccessful IVG cycles and 2 miscarriages, they lose another baby at 28 weeks due to a placental abruption.  With other health issues that crop up during the pregnancy, Zoe is advised that it would be an extremely high risk for her to attempt another pregnancy.  When she asks if she can, Max leaves the session .. and her.<br /><br />Max is a recovering alcoholic who falls off the wagon after the divorce that he asked for.  He is living with his older brother Reid and Reid's wife, Liddy, both born-again evangelicals who attend the Eternal Glory Church, where Clive Lincoln is pastor.  Reid and Liddy have their own fertility issues and have lost five babies pre-birth.  Max and Liddy are good friends, both of them with a fondness for old B-movies, and Reid, wealthy and successful, has always been there to help Max<br /><br />Clive Lincoln runs protests against gay marriage and abortion, and when Reid and Liddy have him over to talk to Max, Max is not receptive.  Then he has an accident while driving ... DUI ... and calls the pastor.  He becomes a member of the church.<br /><br />Vanessa Shaw is a 34-year-old school counselor who has worked with Zoe before on the case of an autistic student at the school.  When Lucy DuBois, a suicidal, depressed teen whose parents attend Eternal Glory Church, proves to be beyond her reach, with parents who don't believe in psychiatry, she convinces Lucy's mother to allow music therapy.  Zoe begins working with Lucy, and a great friendship with Vanessa develops.  Shortly after Zoe goes through another health crisis, their friendship unexpectedly blossoms into something more.<br /><br />Vanessa and Zoe cross the Rhode Island state line to get married in Massachusetts, and during a talk, Zoe remembers that she still has 3 frozen embryos left; she can still have the baby she wanted so badly, only it will be with Vanessa.  When the infertility clinic tells her she will need to get a consent form from Max, she takes the form to him, knowing that it shouldn't be a problem; after all, the reason Max left her is because he didn't WANT any children.<br /><br />Max says he will think about it, and goes to his pastor with his worries about the child living in sin, etc.  (Here's one of the jaw-dropping moments):  Pastor Clive puts a bug in Max's ear about how HE should file a suit for possession of the embryos (can't tell you the rest; it would be a spoiler).<br /><br />Suddenly Wade Preston, a lawyer and evangelical Christian, enters the picture.  This will be a precedent-setting case, so as Zoe is served, dirty tricks abound, some of which made me gasp.  The media is drawn in, and Westboro Baptist Church members enter the picture as well (this is the group that protests at military funerals, saying that soldiers are dying because gays are allowed in the military).<br /><br />While Max, who apparently isn't listening to his conscience, is being used as a publicity ploy, a courtroom revelation regarding the contract with the infertility clinic crops up that could change everything.<br /><br />I LOVE Zoe's mother Dara ... how friggin' Zen she is; how much she loves her daughter ... she is just such an offbeat person, as evidence by the following exchange between her and Zoe:<br /><br />\\"My lawsuit was on Joe Hoffman's radio show,\\" I say.<br />\\"I know,\\" my mother says.  \\"I heard it.\\"<br />\\"You listen to him?\\"<br />\\"Religiously . . . pun intended.  I tune in when I'm on the treadmill.  I've found that, when I'm angry, I walk faster.\\"  She laughs.  \\"I save Rush (Limbaugh) for my abdominal crunches.\\"<br /><br />While Max (hypocritically) commits his own sins while painting himself as a moral, upstanding Christian, he's allowing others to point fingers at Zoe and Vanessa - putting them through the wringer, characterized as immoral reprobates unfit to raise a child ... a child that he knows he can't raise.<br /><br />The novel's point of view alternates, with varying chapters being narrated by Zoe, Max, and Vanessa ... with one more at the end.<br /><br />No matter how you feel about same-sex marriage or homosexuality in general (unless you're someone who starts foaming at the mouth at the thought of it), as a human or Christian, at what point do we stop moralizing and judging others?  This novel, by showing both sides of the debate, will make you think, even if you've never thought about it before.  It is a truly emotional read - you'll smile, laugh, get angry, get sad.  This book makes you FEEL .. and any story that does that is worth the read.<br /><br />QUOTES (from an ARC; may be different in final copy):<br /><br />I usually only do three quotes, but there were just too many good ones to stick to that number<br /><br />You can argue that its a different world now than the one when Matthew Shepard was killed, but there is a subtle difference between tolerance and acceptance.<br /><br />. . . it's pretty clear to me that Max has been chugging whatever Kool-Aid the Eternal Glory church is offering.  There's something just off about him - something Stepford-like.<br /><br />I don't believe you can wake up and suddenly be gay. But I do believe you can wake up and realize that you cannot spend the rest of your life without a certain individual.<br /><br />\\"This is different, Vanessa. On my birthday, my mother still calls me at ten oh three A.M. and screams and pants into the phone to relive the birth experience.\\"<br /><br />\\"Disown her?  Don't be ridiculous. B003UCPEJ2|5.0|I am largely in agreement with the reviews that gave this set five stars.  I was a little worried, given some of the reviews, that some of the CDs might not work, or be blank.  I do not have SCAD capability, so that did not make a difference to me. I have played at least part of each of the 60 CDs, and they all have worked for me.  I am writing a review to try to counter a couple of bad ones I have seen on here.    When I was a kid living in a rural area in the 1960s, my family inherited a bunch of reel to reel tapes my grandfather had collected, which included a number from this set. While I have not seen or heard some of these recordings for 35 years or more, some of these were the reasona I first got into classical music. (Those reel to reel tapes were a lot of work though!)  While it has been fun and nostalgic to see those album covers again, mostly I just can't get over how good most of these CDs sound.  It is amazing to me that given the rather primitive recording technologies available when compared with today's capabilities, how they were able to get such good initial recordings.  Since some of these performances are historic and as good as we are likely to ever get of some of these pieces, it is really fortunate that they are now available and restored such that the audio quality of the recording is not an issue, at least for me, in deciding which version of something to listen to. All I can say is that those RCA engineers back in the late 50s and early 60s must have been amazing.  While this current restoration is very very good, I doubt it could have been accomplished had the original recordings not been as good as they were when initially made.     I would also like to comment on the packaging and how cool I think it looks, and how user friendly it is for such a large number of CDs, when compared to a lot of box sets I have or have seen. The accompanying booklet contains a great amount of information and a lot if not all of the original liner notes.  Itwould be too much to single out any particular CDs, except to say that none are bad, and a lot of them are exceptional.  This was no small project to produce, and I think it deserves a big hats off.  I only wish such a great collection were available for some of my other old favorite non RCA recordings, at such a reasonable price.

0060936223|5.0|Well, hundreds of people have already rated this book, but I wanted to add yet another 5-stars to the pot. I enjoyed this book perhaps as much as any memoir I've recently read. I will admit I knew little more about Patti Smith than her 70s pop hit, \\"Because the Night.\\" Now I know she was first a poet and an artist before she cracked the ranks of rock'n'roll. Knowing that, I probably should not have been surprised at her eloquence in this volume, her memories of being young and in love in NYC in the 60s and 70s. Because this is a book that is, quite simply, beautiful in its language. Smith's portrait of her two-decade friendship and yes, love affair, with artist/photographer Robert Mapplethorpe is an exquisitely tender and nostalgic one, and yet also clear-sighted and realistic. RM was perhaps best-known for his pornographic images of the S&M and rough trade homosexual life. Smith knew all of this, and she admits she couldn't completely understand this dark side of her one-time lover (and former Catholic altar boy), but her descriptions of their long and deep friendship, and especially of Mapplethorpe's final illness and death from AIDS are, in the end, enough to break your heart. Say what you want, JUST KIDS is, more than anything else, a love story.<br /><br />There's a lot of name-dropping here, which might annoy some people, I suppose. But I loved all the connections here - the early days of 60s artists and bands, obscure and famous - The Holy Modal Rounders, Television, Blue Oyster Cult, Todd Rundgren, Andy Warhol, Sam Shepard (one of Smith's lovers for a time) and others. It was undeniably a magical time in pop music and the arts, and Smith's story gives you a fascinating hard-scrabble worm's-eye view of it all. A terrific book, that deserves the NBA for non-fiction it won. I suspect it will be around for a long time.

Edward, on the other hand, has refrained from self-pleasuring for a week, which, for him, is a lengthy abstinence.  He is beside himself with lust, but some of his crude pre-marriage advances caused weeks of sulking on Florence's part, so that he is understandably cautious.  This book is so short that it's hard to say much without giving away the climax, pun intended.  Suffice it to say that there's a major communication breakdown between these two, perhaps reflective of their socio-economic gap.  Florence is an ambitious violinist from a chilly but wealthy family, while Edward has had a rural upbringing and is somewhat directionless and volatile.  I'm not sure what the author is trying to say here, except that perhaps Florence is more buttoned-up and Edward more emotionally transparent because of their backgrounds, but I don't really think those stereotypes lend much to the story.  More to the point is that they both seem to suffer from the common delusion that their partner is a mindreader.  Certainly they have not yet learned that marriage means constantly having to say you're sorry while you gulp down your pride.

B003O86FMW|4.0|It seems to me that the most difficult thing about writing a trilogy must be in striking a coherent balance between the three books.  Too often it seems as if the trilogy is actually written up as one very long novel and then broken up into three parts almost as an afterthought resulting in all three books feeling entirely too dependent upon each other to ever feel like strong individual novels.  The Hunger Games trilogy is not in any way exempt from this problem, but it does manage better than most.<br /><br />In general, Catching Fire is a faster-paced and inevitably more rewarding read than the first novel, as it concentrates much more on giving life and depth to the number of secondary characters both new and old as well as continuing the excellent world-building begun in the first novel without spending a lot of time re-hashing old information.  As before, the story is told solely from Katniss' point of view and thus the motivations and goals or other characters are shrouded in mystery throughout which lends a great deal of tension and suspense that wouldn't be there otherwise. As with the first novel, however, the book also struggles a bit because of Katniss' limited pov which doesn't allow for much development of any characters that she does not directly interact with for extensive periods of time. Never is this weakness more evident than in the development of Gale as a character and the subsequent weakness of the romantic sub-plot. Fortunately, as this sub-plot is not the primary focus of the novel, this doesn't detract to heavily from the novel as a whole, but the book is always at it's weakest when it lingers on Katniss' conflicted feelings for Peeta and/or Gale as a result.<br /><br />Another place where Catching Fire falls down a bit is in it's role as the second book of a trilogy. As is often case with the second novel of a trilogy, Catching Fire often feels like the first part of a larger novel rather than as a solid novel in its own right.  It spends a great deal of time setting up a lot of plot threads most of which, in the end, haven't quite come together thus merely serving to set the stage for the final novel in the trilogy. Consequently, it often seems as if you're seeing the best the series will have to offer without any clear resolution on any of it.<br /><br />All that said, Catching Fire is a hell of a fun read with an fantastic mixture of adventure, romance and intrigue.  The themes, while mature, are never so mature that I would feel uncomfortable in recommending the series for teenagers as well as adults.  Overall, I found Catching Fire to be well worth both my time and money which is a lot more than I can say about most books these days.  If you've read [[ASIN:B002MQYOFW The Hunger Games]], I highly recommend picking it up.a great deal of time setting up a lot of plot threads most of which, in the end, haven't quite come together thus merely serving to set the stage for the final novel in the trilogy. B002MQYOFW|4.0|At its most basic, [[ASIN:0439023521 The Hunger Games]] is about a group of children being forced into a game of survival in a hostile environment where they are required to eliminate the competition in order to survive.  Thus, at its most basic, it is not all that different from [[ASIN:1421527723 Battle Royale: The Novel]] which is really what interested me in reading it in the first place.  That said, once you get past that basic premise the two are very different books though both are really quite excellent.  Like Battle Royale, however, this really isn't the sort of book that will be everyone's cup of tea.  If the basic premise turns you off, this probably isn't the book for you no matter how good I or anyone else says it is.  Because, as the premise suggests, the action is occasionally pretty brutal, the themes are quite mature and not everything is going to turn nicely and not everyone is going to survive.  That said, it's a damn fun read packed with plenty of drama, action and suspense and well worth your time if dystopian fiction with a survival beat is your thing.<br /><br />The Hunger Games takes place in a dystopian future where a single city called the Capital is the seat of government and rules over the twelve districts and the people within them.  The people of the districts are incredibly poor and often starving, forced to spend their lives in the service of the Capital through the production of goods and by laboring in the industry which is the specialty of their particular region.  Many years before the beginning of [[ASIN:0439023521 The Hunger Games]] there was a 13th district which attempted to overthrow the Capital and was summarily destroyed.  As a reminder of the Capital's overwhelming strength, two children from each remaining district are selected to participate in the annual Hunger Games, a game of survival from which only one child of the twenty-four participating will return alive.<br /><br />The first in a trilogy, The Hunger Games works surprisingly well all on its own.  So much so that the remaining two books in the trilogy are more a pleasant surprise than a necessary part of the overall story.  That said, if you enjoyed it as much as I did, you'll almost certainly be excited to dive into the next book to discover the fallout of the events of The Hunger Games.<br /><br />There is a great deal in The Hunger Games to love as the world-building is solid and the pacing, while a bit slow in the beginning, quickly steps up as the contestants for the games are chosen and the real action begins.  Action which is tempered with intrigue as the games are portrayed as being not only a way to keep the Districts in line, but also as the height of entertainment for the citizens of the Capital.  There is a lot of pomp and circumstance that leads up to the actual games which adds an interesting dynamic to both the games and the characters participating in them.<br /><br />As the story is told strictly from a single point of view, that of Katniss of District 12, the development of secondary characters does suffer as Katniss takes very little notice in people she isn't readily invested in.  That said, the entire story being told from her POV does help ratchet up the tension and suspense since the motivations of several other major characters are never entirely clear. As a result what might otherwise be a weakness seems a fair trade-off especially in this first novel where there is so much else going on that knowing too much about the supporting cast might just bog things down.  I imagine it will become a larger issue going forward, but for now it's a pretty minor complaint about an otherwise awesome book.ly well all on its own.  So much so that the remaining two books in the trilogy are more a pleasant surprise than a necessary part of the overall story.  That said, if you enjoyed it as much as I did, you'll almost certainly be excited to dive into the next book to discover the fallout of the events of The Hunger Games. 1594484775|3.0|Hello,    I should preface this review by pointing out that I am an avid Hornby fan. I've read High Fidelity at least 300 times, and the other novels at least 100.  I understand and adore his prose, and I comprehend the themes implied and even try to acknowledge his own experiences through his fiction.    Juliet, Naked, however, is not one of his finer novels. Although it is well crafted, and has a solid base, it feels like it drags on for page after page,  without really going anywhere. The premise is 300 pages long. By the time the plot starts to advance in any meaningful way - to develop the characters  constructed in the awfully long premise - we're less than a hundred pages until the end of the book.    The novel feels like a short story, unnecessarily elongated to form a boring and uneven book that could've been just as easily digested in fifty pages.    Again, I take nothing away from Hornby. He [along with Blake Nelson] is my favourite writer. I can open up High Fidelity at any page and engulf myself in  the genius that is Nick Hornby. Of course, I also got into her darkest years with World Without Tears and Essence, which were quite chilling. After cancelling, I just could not believe what I had done, so I considered downloading the two uptempo songs. Naah! That was equally unfair. It is very obvious that Copenhagen is about her own feelings on the day that she found out that a very close person to her had passed away. She happened to be in icy cold Copenhagen in October when this happened. I can relate to this zombie-like feeling because I was in the French West Indies when I had something similar happen to me. Did I write a lyric about it and share it with the whole world? Absolutely not! Yes, Lucinda, this is great stress relief, but keep these very special songs to yourself. I also don't need to be reminded of how I felt in Martinique way back in December of 1992.<br /><br />The next cut that I skipped was Soldier's Song. Seeing Black is quite uptempo, but questions why someone took their own life. Songs like Ugly Truth and the very haunting Awakening would have one believe that the Essence demons are back until one reads the lyrics a bit closer. She has beat the demons and has come out victorious, thank God!<br /><br />With my favorite seven cuts ripped to my car's iPod, the reduced-size album is much more enjoyable, because all of the humdrum cuts are all interspersed on the album. Try this approach and many of you naysayers just may find a lot to like on this album. Car Wheels or Little Honey it is not, but it is also much better than West. But by all means, every Lucinda fan should at least buy this entire album and avoid the sound clips. The title cut alone is worth the price. B000FQIRXQ|5.0|\\"David Copperfield\\" is something of a modern fairy-tale. The titular character, like so many other heroes, is a boy born into tragedy. His father dies before he his even born, and while he lives a relatively happy life for the first eight years or so with his mother and Peggotty, the kindly servant of the Copperfield family, his fragile existence is shattered when his mother remarries a horrible, controlling man named Edward Murdstone, who becomes the quintessential \\"evil stepfather\\". He and his sister take over the household, taking advantage of Mrs. Copperfield and abusing David mercilessly with corporal punishment. David endures horrific tenures at a boarding school, beatings by his step-father, and the pain of losing his mother to illness before he is sent away to paste labels on jars of bootblack at a factory....and that's just part 1!    Part 2 is nonetheless fraught with troubles, and follows him as he makes his way through adulthood and confronts the tragedies and joys that come with it. The character of David Copperfield has always been one of my favorites from any of Dicken's books. He does not make his own story, but has his story shaped by the actions of those around him. His steadfast resoluteness in the face of adversity is admirable, if not inspiring, and his diligence and aspiration for something more in life, no matter how low he has sunk, ultimately leads him to his own \\"happily ever after\\"; not like Pip in \\"Great Expectations\\", who largely comes to nothing because of his own shortcomings.    The story is typically Dickensian, with dirty London streets, heart-tugging tragedy, and plenty of cruel adults who wreak their vengeance upon innocent children. There are light moments, of course, and as far as plot goes, this one isn't nearly as dark and bleak as \\"Great Expectations\\" or \\"Oliver Twist\\". For one thing, it actually has a good ending for just about everyone, a rarity in most of Dicken's other works.     The BBC evidentially put a ton of time andmoney into this production. Everything looks so authentic, from the sets down to the costumes. Much of it was filmed on location, and even what wasn't still appears in perfect continuity to the time period. The score, composed by Robert Lane and interspersed with several classical pieces, is simply beautiful, and avoids sounding either cheap or overproduced for a film of this scale.    If you've been turned off by previous Masterpiece productions because of their lack of proper casting, than you'll be pleasantly surprised by this one. Everyone is perfect for their parts, and give solid, believable performances. Many will criticize for the abundance of British film royalty, but the way I see it, the reason those people are so famous in the first place is because they're so talented, and when you're trying to adapt a Charles Dickens novel, you need good actors to do the job. Some of the standouts include Maggie Smith as Aunt Betsey Trotwood, Bob Hoskins and Imelda Staunton as the irrepressible Mr. and Mrs. Micawber, Pauline Quirke as Peggotty, and Amanda Ryan as Agnes. Nicholas Lynhurst gives a suitably slimy performance as Uriah Heep, and becomes a villain you want to hate. Ian McKellan is equally as despisable as the vicious headmaster, Mr. Creakle.     As for David Copperfield himself, Daniel Radcliffe gives an astoundingly good performance in the first part of this film as young David. He was only ten at the time, but is as good or better than most of the adults, and plays his part with an incredible amount of pathos and emotion. The looks in his eyes are truly those of an old soul in a child's body. How anyone could even pretend to be cruel to him is beyond me. Because of him, Part 1 is made even more compelling. That may account for the slight let-down feel of Part 2. Oh well. If only they had been able to find an actor as talented as him to play David as an adult, then this production would have been absolutely perfect.    All in all, this is the best PBS Masterpiece production I have ever seen, and certainly one of the best Dickens adaptations. If you like good, well-produced British drama, don't fail to see \\"David Copperfield\\". You won't be sorry!

0156347113|4.0|Loosely associated thoughts on Keynes's \\"General Theory\\"<br /><br />1) The General Theory is a landmark work in the science of economics.  If you agree with the general conclusions or deny them, you cannot deny the influence of the work.  This is the same with Keynes as it is with Hayek or Friedman or Fischer or Marx or Smith.  This work, as the others, are part of the cannon.  Keynes was wrong in places.  He was right in others.  Different schools of thought may disagree on which place is which.<br /><br />2) Part of the power of the work is that it is set up in response to an orthodoxy that is no longer demode.  Poor professor Pigou is beat over the rocks for his poor understanding of economic phenomenon.  Pigou is a minor figure in history were Keynes not to attack him; instead he has an immortality that is undeniable.  The only possible problem here is that Pigou is a straw man, as most contemporary readers will have to rely on Keynes's characterization of Pigou's positions.<br /><br />3)  To get into terms of contemporary financial debate, Keynes was in favor of a financial transition tax, which we would propose as a \\"Tobin Tax\\" nowadays.  Not that I'm a Keynesian, but within the context of capitalism I tend to agree with the economists aligned with his tradition more than the other side(s).<br /><br />4) This is a big point, but Keynes acknowledges that the book is intended for professional economists.  There is a lot of stuff here that is opaque to the lay reader.  S/he might need a class on the topic to walk him/her through the arguments.  I know I had to brush off my calculus to get through large sections.  It might have better to have been on a white board.<br /><br />5)  However, a lot of the stuff is accessible.  Keynes is a good writer.  He had to be, considering the group he ran with.  He was the square in the hippy house, but a certain style is evident in his writing.  Though a large part of the chapters is devoted to the mathematical basis of his argument, he still explains what he is talking about.  This comes at the end of the chapters in surprising little nuggets.  You can't escape his beautiful metaphors, especially his most famous one about the market, where the function is to find \\"what average opinion believes average opinion to be\\".ument, he still explains what he is talking about. I think the book fails to acknowledge that total calorie consumption  and exercise have an important SECOND ORDER effect on body weight,   along with things like stress, mood, environment, etc.    There are more recently discovered ways that your adipose cells can   absorb fatty acids even in the complete absence of insulin   (look up Acylation Stimulating Protein). B001N1DPDE|5.0|Overall, I think The KaBar Becker BK-2 \\"Campanion\\" is an excellent knife. I was very happy to find that KaBar is producing the Becker designs again. I like this knife so much I recently bought a second one for a back up. I bought my first for about $57 from Amazon.com. I think this is an excellent field/utility knife that would also certainly serve well in a survival situation.     It's a very robust knife, just a hair under 1/4 inch thick, full tang*, weighs about a pound or so, holds an edge well, and it's not too hard to sharpen. It's made of 1095 steel, coated with a smooth black protective coating. I have the second version, which has the tang extended slightly beyond the handle into a pommel useful for pounding. This is a very solid knife which holds up well to: cutting, slicing, scraping, chopping, batoning, and even digging, prying, hammering, and cooking. It is heavy.      The original handle scales are black \\"Grivory,\\" which is fiberglass impregnated nylon plastic, I believe. It's very light and strong and should be impervious to weather, but the handle scales are where I have my main criticisms. First, they are a comfortable shape and fit my hands well, but they are quite smooth. This can be easily fixed with some skateboard tape or other wrapping, or by scoring or checking the handle scales with a file or Dremel tool. Second, as many others have noted, the handle scales can come loose a little too easily. The scales are fixed to the knife with three hex-head bolts and nuts. Apparently these are not well-torqued at the factory. This can also be easily fixed in several different ways. Simply using a hex key wrench to tighten the bolts; or wrapping with tape or cord as I mentioned above also works. My solution was to remove the bolts and coat their threads with LockTite, then properly torque them down again. They haven't loosened at all since I did this.     The knife comes with a black Kydex sheath which holds the knife well and includes a nylon belt loopand retention snap strap, although the Kydex holds the knife very securely without the strap. The belt loop will fit belts up to five inches wide and is removable in case you want to attach the Kydex sheath to your gear with its grommets and slots. I believe the sheath would hold the knife securely without the retention strap even in a tip up position.     Finally, I'd like to add that although I'm very please with this knife as it came from the factory, I have modified it a bit. I replaced the handle scales with the Micarta version that KaBar sells about $40. They are a little heavier and of course they add quite a bit to the overall cost of the knife, but they do improve the grip, since they're \\"rougher\\" than the original Grivory scales. They fit perfectly with the original nuts and bolts, but as I said above I used LockTite on the bolts when I installed the Micarta grips. So far I'm very pleased with the improved grip. They also seem to slightly improve the sheath's grip on the knife, which was already very good. Of course, I also think they improve the appearance of the knife, giving it a more \\"organic\\" look and feel. I also added a short 550 cord lanyard.     *I said full tang above, and I believe it does meet this definition, but I did find, when I removed the scales, that the tang has two cutouts between the bolt holes. I suspect this was done to improve the balance of the knife. I don't believe this affects the structural strength of the knife at all.

Lauren Oliver has written an original novel centered around a common concept: love. Delirium is a thought-provoking look into a society that is so fearful of love, that it is actually devoid of it. With the ability to stir up raw emotions, Oliver captures her audience, not just with an enticing storyline, but with her lyrical writing. Readers are bound to get swept into Lena's mind and world, it won't be long before readers themselves begin to feel the touch of deliria.     Told through Lena's perspective, her voice is real and inviting which will ultimately lead readers deeper and deeper into her life, personal thoughts, and the corrupted world she lives in. This is a story that readers will immediately gravitate towards because as similar as it is to the world we live in, it is vastly different. Evenly paced, Oliver builds a vivid world that readers can easily picture in their mind's eye.    Delirium is a heart-wrenching romantic suspense novel that will leave readers gripping their seats waiting to see what will happen to Lena with both fear and anticipation. Oliver really knows how to, not only instill emotion within her audience, but also feed off it at the same time.

But the fact that so many American children come out entitled and without discipline cannot be overlooked.

B004EBT5CU|5.0|Adele just gets better and better with age.  Now, I am a HUGE fan of her debut album `19', and it's a rarity for an artist with such a defining debut to actually come back and surpass all expectations with their sophomore album.  Even Damien Rice, who is above and beyond superb, couldn't top `O' with `9'.  Well, `21' is, quite possibly, the best album I own...and I own `19', so guess what that means?<br /><br />Yup, Adele tops her best with this collection of beautifully focused and executed GEMS.<br /><br />If you aren't familiar with Adele, then you really need to acquaint yourself right away.  From her smoky British accent to the way her voice separates when she reaches the heights of her vocals, piercing the heart as well as the eardrums (in a good way), it's just a total joy to listen to her.  She is one of the best artists working today, delivering a great homage to the late great jazz and blues singers of yesteryear while adding her own modern spin, enough to elevate her material and make her relevant and accessible.<br /><br />Just listen to the blistering opener, `Rolling In the Deep', for further proof that this girl has it all.<br /><br />`21' features a lot of softer moments for Adele.  `Someone Like You' closes the album with one of my favorite songs from the artist.  With a piano laden musical arrangement, Adele laments over the loss of a lover who has moved on with sincerity and beautifully registered remorse.  `Don't You Remember' adds elements of folk rock, giving her a bit of a country vibe (which suits her beautifully).  The way her voice rockets skyward on the chorus is impeccable.  `Take It All' reminds me of `19' a lot.  It is beautifully sung, but I the midst of `21's impeccable lineup, the track feels like filler to me (but that's just me).  Still, soft, sincere and beautiful to the ears.  `Turning Tables' is stunning, totally highlighting Adele's silky voice with a simple yet stirring musical arrangement.  You can feel her frustrations within the relationship she is lamenting, her voice delivers so much emotion.<br /><br />I just LOVE `Set Fire to the Rain'.  It is such a brilliantly crafted track, delivering an almost pop sound throughout the verse structure, emphasizing Adele's accessible vocals (and giving her relevance in this pop obsessed culture) but then she hits the chorus that is so far superior to any and all pop tracks released by these `Pop Princesses' today (the control she manifests on every word is BEYOND stunning).<br /><br />There are still the bouncy jazz throwback songs here, like `Rumour Has It', that just color this album with such playfulness.  I love the apparent Motown influences on `Rumour Has It', a song that is just so much fun.  `He Won't Go' is certainly catchy, and the R&B vibe just settles so nicely on Adele.  I love `I'll Be Waiting', which reminds me a lot of something Anthony Hamilton (love him) and Joss Stone (love her) would conjure up if they ever collaborated.  The soulfulness, almost gospel quality, is just undeniable.<br /><br />And that brings me to the best and worst.<br /><br />The worst, for me, is the cover of `Lovesong', and this isn't to say that it is bad because it isn't.  My only complaint is that I've heard it covered much better by other acts.  In fact, both 311 and Snake River Conspiracy have covered this song with vastly different yet equally brilliant results.  Adele sings it beautifully, but it just seems too simple to really stand out as impressive.  The best, for me, is `One and Only'.  Seriously, the song gives me chills.  I instantly think of the late greats when I hear this song, the aged (vintage) quality of the track just drips all over my eardrums, and that chorus is so pristine, so heart-swelling; I can't help but fall head over heals for it.  The growl in her voice just adds so much emotional umph to the song, and the message is relayed with ease yet and earnestness that swells in her heart and mine.<br /><br />So, I think you know whatI think you should do right about now...BUT THIS ALBUM!<br /><br />For those of you interested; my ranking:<br /><br />1) One and Only<br />2) Rolling In the Deep<br />3) Someone Like You<br />4) Set Fire to the Rain<br />5) Turning Tables<br />6) Rumour Has It<br />7) Don't You Remember<br />8) I'll Be Waiting<br />9) He Won't Go<br />10) Take It All<br />11) Lovesongyou interested; my ranking:    1) One and Only  2) Rolling In the Deep  3) Someone Like You  4) Set Fire to the Rain  5) Turning Tables  6) Rumour Has It  7) Don't You Remember  8) I'll Be Waiting  9) He Won't Go  10) Take It All  11) Lovesong

0525423273|5.0|Reviewed for [...]<br /><br />I'm not sure where I first heard of Anna and the French Kiss, but everything I have heard has been positive. Actually, positively glowing would be more like it. While I don't review many YA books for My Comfy Chair, my curiosity was piqued, so I picked up a copy.<br /><br />At its base structural level, this is pretty standard YA romance fare with heartthrobs and heartbreak and misunderstandings. What raises this book so far above its genre is the sheer appeal of its characters, and the believability of their reactions to the people and situations they face.<br /><br />Anna Oliphant is looking forward to her senior year of high school in Atlanta, but her newly famous father decides to send her to a boarding school in Paris. Rough life, but Anna isn't happy to leave behind her best friend and familiar life. What follows is a blend of humor and melodrama as Anna falls for the gorgeous &Eacute;tienne St. Clair with his to-die-for English accent and his very steady girlfriend. He even seems to like her back, but throughout the tumultuous, emotional year, they can't seem to get on the same page.<br /><br />As I said, standard fare, except that Stephanie Perkins has imbued these characters with all of the anguish, but little of the angst, of real teenagers coming of age and sorting through their feelings for parents, friends and love interests. Instead of rolling your eyes as the latest complication keeps Anna and &Eacute;tienne apart, you find yourself cheering them on.<br /><br />There is enough sexual tension that very young teens might be uncomfortable, but not enough that parents need worry. After all, what Anna longs for is indicated in the title. You'll have to read the book to see if she gets it.<br /><br />I heartily recommend this for teenage girls who will love both the exotic &Eacute;tienne and the city of Paris which provides a backdrop to the story, although not from a tourist point of view, but a teenager in a strange land point of view. I even think teenage boys might be interested, if only to get a glimpse of what the girls around them are really like. Of course, I couldn't blame any parents who picked this up and couldn't put it down again either. I was supposed to save it for a trip, and couldn't stop reading it.<br /><br />Five Stars!to get a glimpse of what the girls around them are really like. 0312370849|2.0|This book is about an interesting and important subject, the holocaust and the response to it by the non-Jewish civilian population in France.  It starts in an interesting manner, with the narration switching back and forth between a contemporary woman, researching the holocaust in France for an English language journal, and a girl of French nationality and Jewish ethnicity during wartime Paris. The little girl, being taken away by the police during a roundup of Jews in Paris helps her younger brother hide in their apartment to avoid being taken away. 0618918248|2.0|For us who were brought up in atheist or agnostic milieus and have since pursued careers in the sciences, Richard Dawkins has done the favor of binding all of our cherished conceits conveniently between the covers of a single volume.  Here we will be reassured that we are of like mind with the 93% of elite and enlightened members of the National Academy of Science who do not believe in a personal god.  It will be averred that our favorite arguments against the existence of such a god remain unassailable.  We will be reminded that the traditional arguments in favor of religion are not only philosophically unsound, they are unscientific.  At the end of this book the sense of self-satisfaction is complete.<br /><br />But this was not the author's objective.  \\"If this book works as I intend,\\" he says, \\"religious readers who open it will be atheists when they put it down.\\"  One anticipates, then, breaking the horse gently, gradually tempering him to bridle and bit.  But just a few pages later Dawkins comments on \\"the weakness of the religious mind,\\" a characterization that seems poorly calculated to win such a mind to his point of view.  A short distance further on he refers to the Hebrew god as \\"the Old Testament's psychotic delinquent.\\"  This is among the gentler salutations found throughout the book.  In fact as you read this thing it's hard to overcome the impression that rather than persuade the religious, he seeks instead to bludgeon them.<br /><br />One of the author's gripes about religion, aside from its failure to conform to rationalism, is \\"society's exaggerated respect\\" for it.  Dawkins' exemplar of this is the 2006 case of the Danish cartoons of Muhammad.  He recounts the apparent conspiracy to whip up resentment among Muslims worldwide and the resulting riots and abuse, including murder, of Christians and Westerners.  \\"In response to all this frenzied pandemonium,\\" comments Dawkins, \\"decent liberal newspapers deplored the violence and made token noises about free speech.  But at the same time they expressed `respect' and `sympathy' for the deep `offence' and `hurt' that Muslims had `suffered'.\\"  Dawkins then wonders at the \\"disproportionate privileging of religion.\\"  \\"What is so special about religion,\\" he asks, \\"that we grant it such uniquely privileged respect?\\"<br /><br />This is not hard to figure out.  It was not a case of the \\"privileging of religion\\" generally but of Islam in particular.  And the responses of newspapers were not out of societal regard for religious faith, Islamic or otherwise, but rather concern over provoking further violence among Muslims and putting more people at risk.  These were editorial decisions, and it can be debated whether or not they were prudent, but they are not evidence of privilege.<br /><br />The absence of special privilege for religion is particularly true in America.  Dawkins is British, and things might be quite different in Britain, but here in America we have the First Amendment, pursuant to which we say and print things critical of religious faith, religious institutions, and religious people all the time, e.g., Dawkins' book.  It is such a commonplace, in fact, that it hardly merits notice.  We have even gone so far as to have displayed works of \\"art\\" such as Andres Serrano's \\"Piss Christ,\\" a photograph of corpus and crucifix submerged in the shutterbug's own urine.  Despite the potential religious offensiveness of this image, it was not suppressed.  In fact it was supported partially at public expense, and the debut was widely reported in the press.  (Artistic acclaim was glowing, religious acclaim less so, but still Christians somehow refrained from worldwide riot.)<br /><br />The difference between public attitudes toward religion in America and Britain is the subject of a particular puzzle for Dawkins.  \\"[T]he United States, founded in secularism, is now the most religiose country in Christendom, while England, with an established church ... is among the least.  Iam continually asked why this is, and I do not know.\\"<br /><br />This is not hard to figure out either.  While the front-line Anglican clergyman of Britain has become (Dawkins quoting one Giles Fraser, vicar) a \\"tea-drinking, gentle eccentric\\" who \\"represented a type of religion that didn't make non-religious people feel uncomfortable,\\" there remain in America clergy from numerous sects who sincerely and energetically believe.  If Britain's establishment clergy demonstrate they lack commitment, you can hardly expect more from the laity.  In contrast, many American clergy are eager to sweep up the leavings of unserious churches and make of them believers.  And despite the scientific advances of our time, a fervent promise of salvation or call to moral purpose, however fantastical, will persuade far more people than will an appeal to morally indifferent science.  Benighted these converts might be, but if Prof. Dawkins seeks to persuade them, he might wish to take into account some results from the science of psychology.<br /><br />But he would rather demonstrate the obtuseness of religion by recounting the long history of religious corruption, the foolishness of belief, and the power of rational counter-argument.  All of us who have tried this know how easy it is and how pleasing to press a foot on the neck of an intellectual inferior.  All of us who have tried it also know how easy it is for this tactic to give offense.  Which may account for some of the \\"hatred and misunderstanding of atheists\\" that Dawkins cites, documentation of which is kept in a database maintained by the Freethought Society of Greater Philadelphia.  One shouldn't hate atheists, of course.  Disagree with them, sure; debate them if you have time and inclination.  Likewise one shouldn't hate theists, and perhaps Dawkins doesn't, but as is evident in this book, he certainly holds their views in solid and public contempt.  It does nothing to lessen any supposed hatred and misunderstanding of atheists that their most prominent spokesman makes himself obnoxious.am continually asked why this is, and I do not know.\\"<br /><br />This is not hard to figure out either. 0743496736|2.0|So I admit, this book was a page-turner for me. I read it quickly and I found the plot interesting and not completely overdone, and the writing wasn't terrible. After I read it a second time, though, it really started grating on me.    I have two main complaints with it, and they were big ones. The first was that Picoult thought she was being deep and profound, and you could *tell.* Each chapter, each section, ended with a line that was unnecessary, a comment that didn't contribute to the story or flow at all but was put in there to emphasize that this was a thought provoking, insightful piece. However, these lines did was annoy me. I like stories that really make you think, not stories that repeat their points over and over and want to look like they're saying something.    The other issue was even bigger, and that was how heavily the author relied on cliches and stereotypes. I am a high schooler. I went to a public school. And high school is nothing like she described. It was just completely unrealistic. Yes, bullying exists, and yes, it sucks if you have no friends, but the social stratification is NOT this distinct and NOT this superficial and NOT this set in stone. Popular kids are popular because they have a lot of friends, and as a general rule of thumb, people who are total jerks do not get that many friends. I admit, maybe it would have happened as she portrayed it in Middle School, but by high school most people have matured and are at least more subtle about their bullying. The stereotype of Alex (the judge, the mother) was also tiresome. Yes, I understand she doesn't know how to talk to her kid. This isn't an uncommon problem. And yes, I understand Josie doesn't feel she has an identity. Well you know what? 90% of America feels the same way, and they deal with it. While this could have made the characters easy to relate to, they never did anything but whine, and so it simply made them annoying.    It's not a terrible read, really. It's just not exceptional either.

Actual gladiators--to judge by ancient drawings--seem to have been bulkier men with thicker waists. 1856697061|5.0|I purchased the first volume of Pattern Magic, and liked the approach the author takes to revising a sloper into an innovative geometric clothing design. I could draft a sloper but didn't know how to use the sloper to create a fashion design pattern until reading Pattern Magic. (A sloper is a basic fitted clothing pattern used as a basis for fitting fashion patterns.) This book filled a gap for me. By the way, Pattern Magic is translated from Japanese, so the format may be different from other sewing books you have read.<br /><br />I bought Pattern Magic 2 because I found the first volume so helpful. The approach is the same in both books; taking a sloper with darts taped closed, planning the geometric design for the garment and marking the design lines on the sloper, cutting the sloper so that it lies flat (this creates darts/pleats), and drafting the fashion pattern on a fresh sheet of paper by tracing around the resulting cut sloper.<br /><br />The last few pages of the book includes some small-scale slopers which can be copied or traced if you want to practice with paper on a small scale (the first volume also includes these mini-slopers).<br /><br />This book illustrates different geometric and pleated shapes for clothing/fashion design. The designs are a basis for helping the reader understand how to adapt a 2-dimentional sloper into a 3-dimensional clothing design. This is a book for advanced learning on how to create your own original designs for patterns that fit; it is not a book of ready-to-stitch patterns, instead it is a book of examples of how to transform slopers into patterns. The examples shown are detailed and illustrated.<br /><br />Overall, this is a helpful book for a clothing designer or advanced or professional dressmaker who wants to go beyond slopers. There are good beginner books on drafting slopers, so if you have not drafted a sloper, check out a more basic book detailing how to draft a sloper. The clothing designs shown in the first volume are different from those shown in this volume. I do not have a preference between these two volumes, but I particularly liked the pleating designs which are detailed in Pattern Magic 2.me are different from those shown in this volume. I do not have a preference between these two volumes, but I particularly liked the pleating designs which are detailed in Pattern Magic 2.

To me, Freedom has touches of James Jones, Mailer and Roth, and lots and lots of Oprah, Ricki Lake and Roseanne.  Two sections are in the style of a woman undergoing analysis. Her therapeutic self-evaluation becomes the novel's.  Morality isn't equal justice or reciprocal liberty the way men have traditionally argued it, but the weighing and weighting of feelings: are yours authentic, borrowed, warped, reversed as to subject or object? whose feelings have been hurt and how much by withholding, abandoning, seducing, betraying? and; blame is measured in both motives and pain. The characters' arguments are endless as those of a child who knows intuitively how \\"maybe\\" proves everything and nothing.  Truth is in psychological causes, not political ones; and, real men melt, like Turandot.  A few stock villains, some with star turns, drop names like \\"noble lies\\" and allude to ends justifying means, but sweet talk (and cookies) carry the action forward, transcending such problems. 0756404738|5.0|Most books, even very enjoyable books, have obvious flaws. I've read hundreds of fantasy books and it's common to find a book with a great story idea but the author isn't really a masterful writer. Or a book has solid writing but the characters are shallow stereotypes. So I was blown away by [[ASIN:0756405890 The Name of the Wind]], the first volume of this trilogy. The ideas were compelling, the world and its various cultures were well thought out, the characters leapt off the page, and there was even a fair amount of dry humor. I was pleased when I heard that the other two books of the trilogy were already written. I figured they'd be released shortly thereafter.    Well, it turns out that \\"already written\\" was a bit overgenerous. Patrick Rothfuss had a lot of editing and rewriting to do. I fumed at the delay because I was so enthralled by the first volume. Eventually I started to worry: how could the second volume match the standard of excellence set by the first volume?     I'm pleased to say that, if anything, Wise Man's Fear is a stronger volume than Name of the Wind. With the character of Kvothe already well established, Rothfuss is able to dive in to some of young Kvothe's most exciting adventures. He does a great job splitting the story between more mundane relationship-driven parts and the big payoffs of splashy magic and exciting fights. The story never bogs down, the pacing is wonderful. We see Kvothe using and expanding his skills and knowledge in ways that are entirely consistent with what has come before. There's no cheap \\"and suddenly I found the magic sword\\" deus ex machina moments.     A recurring theme is to juxtapose legend vs. reality. There's the legend of Kvothe himself contrasted with his current mundance existence as Kote the innkeeper. There's the legend of the Chandrian and the Amyr, and Kvothe's frustratingly slow path to find out any facts at all about them apart from children's stories. The legend of the fearsome Adem mercenaries vs. the slow reveal of the truth behind their fighting prowess and their mysterious silence. And of course the legend and reality of the fae Falurian, whom no mortal man can resist.     He has done such a good job setting up various sub-plots that I can't imagine how he can satisfactorily resolve them all in the final volume of the series. I have to admit I wouldn't mind if the series stretched to a fourth volume. It's simply that good.

B004LGPBZI|4.0|I wasn't entirely sure what to expect when I got my copy of 'Different Gear, Still Speeding'. I had previously heard the first single, 'Bring The Light', and was not particularly impressed with it, but not turned off by any means. Then I heard 'Four Letter Word' and 'The Roller' and thought they were fantastic rockers that would define this album. To my unfortunate surprise this was not the case.<br /><br />I am a long-time Oasis fan and love every single entry in their catalog, and normally I would not even mention Oasis in this review and look at Beady Eye in its own respect. However, I cannot shake Liam's howling for months prior to the record's release how 'Different Gear, Still Speeding' will be \\"better than Oasis\\" and that soon enough people will be naming their children 'Beady Eye'. The album just didn't live up to Liam's hype, but I honestly never expected it to. Oasis is a definition of rock & roll music, while Beady Eye, though containing veteran musicians, is still in its infancy.<br /><br />Putting aside the Oasis/Beady Eye comparisons and reviewing this album as originally intended, it still came as a slight disappointment from the band. Gem and Andy are both excellent songwriters and Liam's voice has noticeably regained its power since the latter touring days of Oasis. There are some true gems on this album, but a lot that feels like filler. 'Wigwam', the longest track on the album clocking in at 6:39, still manages to be forgettable.<br /><br />It is very clear what this album was trying to be: a hark back to 50's and 60's rock & roll oldies, and there's nothing wrong with that - those were defining eras of rock music evolution. However, what lacks with 'Different Gear, Still Speeding' is the same memorability of those oldies that influenced this album. Everything sounds very polished, unlike the dirty, raw sound of (sorry, again) Oasis that made us fall in love with them.<br /><br />In sum, I really wanted this album to blow my head off. It failed to do that, but it still gave me some good toe-tappers and my fix for oldies-inspired rock & roll. Beady Eye will never rise out of the shadow of Oasis, but they do not have to in order to make great music. There's a bit to take away from this album, and a bit to leave behind. Ultimately, I am disappointed it didn't live up to Liam's hype and my hopes of a truly rocking album. Still a solid record and miles above the majority of mainstream rock today, which is why it still gets a 4/5 from me in spite of its underwhelming nature.<br /><br />I'd recommend a thorough listen, but if you are an Oasis fan you may be confused and disappointed with this one. If you can treat Beady Eye as a separate entity as I have, you will find enjoyment from these songs. You may not be swept out from under your feet, but you'll bob your head back and forth; I'll still be playing this one time and time again. B001E45XT4|2.0|After viewing a bunch of reviews for the Gamecom 367 closed ear gaming headset, I was pretty excited about getting such a great deal on what seemed like a universally praised set of headphones. I was not happy with what I received to say the least.    Sound  The sound on these things are absolutely horrific. Even if you are not an audiophile, you'll immediately notice that THERE IS ALMOST NO BASS. I thought it might have been a driver issue or my laptop had its audio settings set on \\"0\\" for bass, but it was neither; it was the headphones itself. At medium frequency to high frequency sounds, the headphones do a mediocre job. Yes, you CAN hear most sounds when gaming and they'll benefit you as opposed to having no headphones. But I own a cheap pair of Sony headphones (which I now still use to play counterstrike and ps3 games) that I had purchased back in 2005 and those possess better sound quality than the Gamecom 367's.    Comfort  An even weaker feature to these headphones are how they feel when you put them on. The cups are locked in place and unable to rotate in ANY DIRECTION, a serious design flaw. After 20-30 minutes of wearing, my head hurt even after trying to adjust the cups numerous times. I had seen the tigerdirect review where the guy said he had worn them for 3 hours straight with no discomfort. Absolute bull. No matter how thin your head is around the temples, the cups literally CLAMP onto the sides of your head. I've gotten dizzy after wearing them after an hour. There is absolutely no way anybody can be comfortable wearing these headphones. The velvet padding is a plus, though barely helpful since they're smashed against your temples.    Microphone  This is perhaps the only good thing about these headphone (aside from the velvet pads), but there is no way in hell it'll make the headphones worth buying. The sound quality on the microphone is great and when I teamchat with my buddies, they can hear me just fine. I even tried recording myself with the microphone on this headset and there was practically no background noise. This is a plus but the only plus.    All in all, I am SHOCKED by how many people consider these headphones a decent set of headphones for its price. It has horrible sound quality and only people with thin heads can find any comfort by the deathgrip the cups have on your head.    BUYER BEWARE. STAY AWAY FROM THESE HEADPHONES.

B000Q6GUW0|5.0|Having finished The Wire and BSG I had been looking for another television series to get into. A friend recommended Dexter. I must admit that the initial premise didn't strike me as something I would like - a show where a serial killer is the main character - how was that going to work?  Well it works quite well, though the writers do have to cheat a little - more on this in a minute.  At present I'm half way through Series one and I already know I'll be getting series 2 - why?    First, the writing is superb - American networks have been making some amazing series for the last decade or so - The Sopranos and The Wire being probably the best tv I have ever seen, there's also been Lost, BSG and Mad Men. One of the big advantages tv enjoys over standalone movies is that the writers can spend more time developing characters and filling in back-story to make it a richer viewing experience. If it's handled well it can be very rewarding and Dexter certainly shines in this department.    Second, acting. Superb throughout but obviously Michael C. Hall carries the show - if he can't make the main character work then the whole house of cards falls - he succeeds brilliantly making Dexter a perfect study of conflicted urges.    Third, plot. The first season story arch concerns the ice-truck killer but it is really also about introducing the character of Dexter. How do you make a serieal killer someone who you want to spend an hour each week with?  In my view, as mentioned above a certain amount aof cheating is required.    Cheat 1: You never actually see Dexter torturing and killing anyone - you see the lead up and the aftermath but not the act itself. Would we the viewer find Dexter such an endearing character if we saw his violent acts play out on the screen? I doubt it.    Cheat 2: Dexter's victims are portrayed as being the embodiment of pure evil. It's made clear to the viewer that anyone who Dexter kills deserves it. Reality is rarely so black and white.    Ultimately, Iguess that even though I recognise I'm being manipulated somewhat by the writers, I'm enjoying the show too much to care. I'd have no hesitation recommending Dexter. It's a smart funny, well written and superbly acted show that deserves the critical praise it's been getting on these pages.

0061353248|4.0|Dan Ariely, the James B. Duke Professor of Psychology and Behavioral Economics at Duke University, writes about \\"the hidden forces that shape our decisions\\" in Predictably Irrational. This book is not as diverse as some of the other behavioral economics books out there, but Ariely makes up for breadth with depth. Many of the subjects tackled are explored in detail.<br />Ariely writes about how he first became interested in the field of economics. You can skip the introduction to most economics book, but Ariely's story is worth reading. Ariely received third degree burns on over 70% of his body as an eighteen year old. His experiences in the hospital, including three years of recovery and therapy, convinced him to study what shapes our decisions.<br /><br />For the most part, the subjects Ariely tackles will keep your interest. Some of the broader financial subjects, like supply and demand, could have been tightened. The information is good but too much time is spent explaining the experiments used to prove it.<br /><br />Ariely spends a lot of his time in the more traditional economics arena, albeit with examples that make the subject interesting to anyone. Unfortunately for the reader, the most fascinating subjects are also the shortest. There are at least two chapters that I will refer to in the future, one on the influence of arousal and the other on the context of our character.<br /><br />In Chapter Six, Ariely tackles arousal. Ariely concludes that we make bad decisions when we are controlled by lust. Not just bad decisions either . . . he shows how right and wrong become very gray when we are trapped in temptation. He offers two solutions, but focuses in on the second. He concludes that the best way to avoid bad decisions is to avoid putting ourselves in that situation. Some parents have decided that once they teach their kids to say \\"no\\" that their job is done. This book easily disproves that theory . . . it is often the parents job to say \\"no\\" so their children will be protected from uncontrollable situations.<br /><br />Another great chapter covered the influence of the Ten Commandments on cheating. Long story short, reflecting on any part of the Ten Commandments was enough to stop cheating. Ariely goes on to test the same with different honor codes and has similar results. The results seemed to have surprised the experimenters. Right and wrong prove to be powerful influencers . . . there is much to reflect on here.<br /><br />Books like this are important for people of all walks of life to read. This book is recommended for anyone who is interested in why we behave the way we do.uncontrollable situations.    Another great chapter covered the influence of the Ten Commandments on cheating. The turnabout then works very well indeed.<br /><br />Changez has returned to Pakistan from the USA, where he both studied and worked. He is in conversation with a foreign visitor to his country. Apparently they are sitting in a caf&eacute;. The visitor is probably an American but, surprisingly, the conversation is entirely one-sided. Basically, Changez tells his life story, eventually relating in detail the conspiring events that led him to his current preoccupations and status.<br /><br />He was the child of an upper class family in Lahore. He was a bright thing from the start and when the time was right a place at Princeton beckoned. He excelled and was offered a job with a business consulting group, where he learned much more than merely contemporary jargon. He also fell head over heels in love with an American girl, herself a gifted student with a desire to write. She wanted to tell stories, beginning perhaps with one featuring herself and describing her former boyfriend's struggle with terminal illness. Initially at least she seems newly besotted with her new Pakistani friend, with Changez's unexpected and wholly foreign politeness, good manners and dress sense all creating favourable impressions.<br /><br />The silent listener absorbs all this without comment as he and Changez await their food in a Lahore restaurant. Everything looks rosy for our graduate and the listening tourist seems to respond to the raconteur's story. The Reluctant Fundamentalist's tone changes abruptly as the world and the individual's place in it seem to need reinterpretation. As things turn out, Changez returns to Pakistan, where he takes up teaching. And still our listener absorbs the story without response.<br /><br />The book's denouement is both surprising and satisfying. The form that has seemed to be a handicap suddenly contributes to the experience. We are left with an enigmatic, open ending where surely something will happen. But in a succinct and sophisticated way it addresses and comments on some complex issues. Its methods are both sympathetic and involving. Its efforts convince the reader without being didactic. It is thus a significant achievement.

B002ZG980U|4.0|One of the things that attracts some people to science fiction (and repels others) is that its stories are usually based on \\"WHAT IF--?\\" premises that are NOT parts of our \\"normal reality.\\" Much early \\"sci-fi\\" or \\"SF\\" was disparaged as crap because the premises overwhelmed both the story arcs and the characters--and the writing also was often very poor. In recent decades, a better balance has been found by hundreds of authors and dozens film makers, and both the TERMINATOR series and ALIEN series, for instance, are now highly respected classics.<br /><br />INCEPTION, I felt, is better than average but has at least three weaknesses--no, make that four--no, five. For one thing, the story arc is trite and its ending derivative (think Terry Gilliam's BRAZIL, 1985). For another, at its own \\"inception\\" we viewers are given material that coyly/pseudo-artfully prefigures some ending scenes but does not logically hold up (Ken Watanabe as an old man in BOTH spots). Thirdly, very often the exposition of the \\"What If--?\\" is clunkier than 20 speed bumps in a new housing development--the action grinds to a halt while one \\"expert\\" explains some new aspect of Dream Management 101 to another \\"expert,\\" just so we viewers can eavesdrop and become informed. Fourthly, we really are NOT made to care much either way about the success or failure of the \\"mission\\" (a kind of caper involving a sub-category of industrial espionage) NOR about the happiness or misery of the main character, a somewhat charmless widower who wants to live with his young children (played by Leonardo DiCaprio). And the back-stories of the other characters are non-existent, so of course we don't care either way about them. And fifthly, the whole notion of creating the \\"inception\\" of an idea in a business rival's head rests on a shakier foundation than trickle-down economics: any kernel of an idea put into anyone's brain can sprout and grow in any of 57-million directions--NOT just the one narrow direction that is desired by the paymaster (played by Ken Watanabe).<br /><br />On the PLUS side--this film has fast-action and good CGI special effects in many places. It IS a decent mind-game with four levels of action that flash round-&-round in kaleidoscopic fashion--and then go to a fifth level. Night Shyamalan's THE LAST AIRBENDER.<br /><br />But I would still sooner rewatch STRANGER THAN FICTION (Will Ferrell, Helen Mirren, Dustin Hoffman). The only reason I put INCEPTION back into my DVD player the next day and sped past scene after scene to the end again--was to satisfy my curiosity--as to how LONG it ran. Superbly acted, beautifully directed, scored, and executed, no movie depicts the socialization of \\"family values\\", the American Dream, and gender roles in such an exciting, fantastical way.  Of course, it goes on to touch base with other hot issues like racism and generation gap, and there it arguably  starts to lose its potency, but no matter who you are or what issue you're interested in, there's something to take away from the powerful imagery and storyline that debunks the \\"ideal life\\" depiction that is shoved down American throats via 1950's television.<br /><br />Because color plays such a crucial role in the success of this film, Pleasantville is unsurprisingly a must-own Blu Ray.  I mean, the entire movie is based on suddenly seeing the world in high definition color - it's like a BD fanatic's fantasy.  It's also obvious the filmmakers were excited about releasing the film in HD because the transfer appeared to be taken at least somewhat seriously.  I won't say it's the greatest, most stunning transfer I've ever seen, but it's certainly powerful enough that you FEEL the 1080p working its magic.  Much of the movie is in black and white, but still grays are exquisitely decipherable, whites are bright and crisp, and darks are appropriately deep and textured.  When color does start to appear it does so with a splash, crash, and bang.  Reds are SO rich and greens appear alive.  Blue is vast and yellow is is particularly captivating.  The full experience of Pleasantville practically DEMANDS Blu Ray technology.  It's hard to imagine I ever watched this movie on a standard DVD, you miss so much of the magic and power a single color can have when you see it in standard definition.<br /><br />My overall opinion of the Blu Ray, having not bothered watching any special features (though there are some interesting ones, including audio commentaries), is that it is simply a must-own.  I'm sure someday down the road there will be a 20th anniversary edition or something that will have a slightly better transfer, but I don't think it's worth waiting for.  I consider myself a bit of a Blu Ray snob (I refuse to watch DVDs anymore) and it would be hard to imagine seeing the movie in much better clarity.  It looks gorgeous, the aspect ratio is 1:85:1 which means the picture will fill the entire (wide)screen, the audio is lossless and impressive, and most importantly this Type of movie is just MEANT for BD. Independent films are one thing, but major ones released by bigger studios are even a bigger deal when this occurs. I think that's one of the reasons Inception was so great. Creativity and intelligence are two things that are usually lacking from blockbusters. The Adjustment Bureau is a film that has a bit more to it than you're probably expecting. Based on \\"The Adjustment Group,\\" a short story by Philip K. Dick (Blade Runner, Total Recall, Minority Report), The Adjustment Bureau has probably already caught your attention either because you're a fan of Dick's work and/or the movies that were adapted from it.<br /><br />The film's charm is definitely in its explanation for things. The way the bureau works and how they function is a wonder in itself. You'll never look at a door or a man wearing a hat the same way again after viewing the film. Perhaps the most interesting is Thompson's explanation of how events in history like The Great Depression and The Holocaust came about. The story is very imaginative and different from the norm, which is always a fantastic change in pace when it comes to film. At the same time though, those who like having absolutely everything explained to them will probably be disappointed. The films surely have their similarities, but The Adjustment Bureau deserves to be given a chance. I think it'll surprise a lot of viewers.    The Adjustment Bureau is easily the smartest live-action film to be released in the first few months of 2011. With a solid cast, a hefty helping of creativity, and just an absorbing experiencing overall, The Adjustment Bureau is bound to leave a lasting impression when it comes time to look back on the best films of the year. The film leaves you questioning how much of your life is really left to fate and chance. Films seem to leave an impression that lasts longer if it makes you think or strikes a cord or hits close to home in some capacity. This film does that and then some. It comes highly recommended even if you have your reservations about it.

B003Y5H53S|5.0|Director Gore Verbinski has put together quite the filmography over the years. His first feature film was the family comedy Mousehunt, which he followed up with the R-rated action comedy The Mexican. He also jumped on the successful remake bandwagon before the trend really took off with The Ring. It was the Pirates of the Caribbean films that teamed the director with the hottest actor in Hollywood today; Johnny Depp. Perhaps it's because those films made over a billion dollars at the box office or because they just had fun working together or a little bit of both that Depp was chosen to voice a talking chameleon in Verbinski's bizarre yet spectacular animated adventure known as Rango.<br /><br />Rango isn't your average animated film. That fact will become abundantly clear during Rango's opening monologue amongst his \\"friends.\\" The film is actually more adult than any of the trailers let on. Within the first ten minutes of the film, Rango has a rather lengthy conversation with some fresh roadkill. In addition to that, the last half of the film is much darker than the first half. Maybe it's the countless number of bats with gatling guns strapped to them, Rattlesnake Jake being one of the most menacing animated villains in years, the film using its fair share of both \\"hell\\" and \\"damn\\" quite a few times, the film not shying away from the use of nooses, or, God forbid, animated characters smoking, but Rango just doesn't feel like an everyday, run-of-the-mill film put out by Nickelodeon.<br /><br />Rango also wears its western references on its sleeve. The old time saloons, tumbleweeds, stare downs before a gunfight, and a town's utmost desire for both a sheriff and something to believe in are proof of that. But perhaps it's Timothy Olyphant's cameo appearance as The Spirit of the West that is both the biggest homage to westerns you could possibly think of and the biggest surprise of the film (at least as far as his appearance goes). Well it's either that or the Fear and Loathing in Las Vegas reference. Both are equally amazing.<br /><br />This has the atmosphere of an animated film that was made for adults. It's very off-balanced in the best kind of way, but a lot of the references and humor are sure to go over a child's head. Some of the characters in the film talk really fast (mostly just Rango and Beans at times) and while Rango is goofy enough to make the kids laugh, the subject content involving the town of Dirt certainly seems to be aimed towards a more mature sense of humor.<br /><br />Rango is the first animated film from Industrial Light & Magic, the special effects company that did computer generated effects for the first three Star Wars films and the effects for the T-1000 in Terminator 2 among countless others. The film looks phenomenal. There were times when Rango looked like he was walking in an actual desert. While the characters weren't quite as detailed as the owls in Legend of the Guardians, they still looked incredibly realistic or as realistic as talking animals could possibly be.<br /><br />Rango is one of the most eccentric animated films you'll ever have the pleasure of sitting through. Its homage to westerns combined with its explosive action sequences, an endless amount of hilarity, tender and sentimental moments that actually make you feel sorry for a talking lizard, and even a little bit of romance pretty much has all your bases covered as far as genres are concerned. Rango is a dark, witty, and entertaining ride that's also fairly mature for an animated film. All in all, Rango is easily the best movie of 2011 so far.s Vegas reference. Both are equally amazing.
